Taharah — Purification
beginner
6 min
The Seven Purifying Waters
Listing the seven types of water permissible for purification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the seven types of water permissible for purification and explain why each is considered mutlaq (absolute) water.

Core Idea
Seven waters are purifying: any water that is 'mutlaq' (absolute) is valid for wudhu and ghusl.

This lesson presents the foundational Hanafi principle that any water that remains in its original, unadulterated state (mutlaq) is purifying. The classical texts enumerate seven specific sources, but the underlying legal criterion is that the water has not been mixed with anything that changes its name or essence. Understanding these seven waters provides a concrete anchor for the broader category of mutlaq water.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the list
Commit the seven waters to memory: rainwater, sea water, river water, well water, melted snow, melted hail, and spring water.
These are canonical; they appear in most Hanafi mutun.
2
Identify the common legal category
All seven are examples of mutlaq water (absolute water), which is the only type that is legally purifying (mutahhir).
3
Understand the grammatical distinction
The construct phrases (idafah) in these terms are for definition (ta'rif), not restriction (taqyid). This means 'water of the sea' is still called 'water' without the qualifier, unlike 'rosewater' which is no longer simply 'water'.
This point preserves the purifying status of these waters.
4
Recognize the exception for melted salt
The author specifically excludes melted salt from the list – salt water (as a solid that melts) is not purifying, unlike melted snow or hail which are pure.
The opposite melting behavior (salt melts in winter, water freezes) indicates a different essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المياه التي يجوز التطهير بها سبعة مياه : ماء السماء وماء البحر، وماء النهر، وماء البئر، وماء ذاب من الثلج والبرد، وماء العين. وماء البحر التنصيص عليه دفعاً لمظنة توهم عدم جواز التطهير به؛ لأنه مرّ منتن كما توهم ذلك بعض الصحابة، ومن الناس من كره الوضوء من البحر الملح؛ لحديث ابن عمر ه: أنه قال: "لا يركب البحر إلا حاج أو معتمر أو غاز في سبيل الله؛ فإن تحت البحر ناراً، وتحت النار بحراً" تفرد به أبو داود، وكان ابن عمر لا يرى جواز الوضوء به، ولا الغسل عن جنابة، وكذا روي عن أبي هريرة. وماء النهر: قد يحرك، وهو مجرى الماء. ذاب من الثلج احترز به عن الذي يذوب من الملح؛ لأنه لا يطهر؛ يذوب في الشتاء ويجمد في الصيف عكس الماء. والبرد: بفتح الباء الموحدة والراء المهملة ماء العين [وهو الذي يتفجر من الأرض لازدياده في داخلها] اعلم أن الإضافة في هذه المياه للتعريف لا للتقييد والفرق بين الإضافتين صحة إطلاق الماء على الأول دون الثاني؛ إذ يصح بأن يقال لماء الورد: "هذا ماء من غير قيد بالورد بخلاف ماء البير؛ لصحة إطلاقه فيه.
The waters with which purification is permissible are seven waters: rainwater, sea water, river water, well water, water melted from snow and hail, and spring water. And [the mention of] sea water is specified to repel the suspicion of it not being permissible to purify with it, because it is salty and foul-smelling, as some Companions thought. And among people, there are those who disliked performing ablution with salty sea water due to the hadith of Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said: 'None should sail the sea except a pilgrim, an Umrah performer, or a warrior in the path of Allah, for under the sea is fire, and under the fire is a sea.' It was narrated exclusively by Abu Dawud. Ibn Umar did not see the permissibility of performing ablution with it, nor bathing from janabah, and similarly it is narrated from Abu Hurairah. River water: it may be pronounced with a vowel movement [nahar] and it is the course of water. [Melted from] snow is a distinction from that which melts from salt, because it [salt] does not purify; it melts in winter and freezes in summer, opposite to water. Hail: with a fatha on the ba and ra'. Spring water: it is that which gushes from the earth due to its increase inside it. Know that the idafah (construct phrase) in these [terms] is for definition (ta'rif) not restriction (taqyid). The difference between the two idafahs is the validity of applying [the word] 'water' to the first without [the second]; for example, one can say of rosewater 'this is water' without the qualifier 'rose', unlike well water, because it is valid to call it water without restriction.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage presents the classic Hanafi ruling on the seven waters that are legally pure and purifying (mutahhir). The list includes: rain, sea, river, well, melted snow/hail, and spring water. The author immediately addresses a potential doubt about sea water: despite its saltiness and odor, some early authorities (like Ibn Umar and Abu Hurairah) considered it unsuitable. The commentary quotes a hadith (though weak, as only Abu Dawud narrated it) used by those who disliked sea water. However, the Hanafi position (following the majority of scholars) is that sea water is perfectly permissible for purification, and the mention here confirms that. The author clarifies that 'melted from snow' excludes salt, as salt water is not purifying. He explains the grammar: the idafah (construct) in these terms is for definition (like 'water of the sea' simply identifies the type) not restriction (like 'rosewater', which restricts the meaning). Well water can still be called water without qualifier, unlike rosewater. Thus all these waters are considered absolute (mutlaq) water in the legal sense, suitable for purification.

Key Terms
ماء السماء (ma' al-sama'): Rainwater, which falls from the sky.
ماء البحر (ma' al-bahr): Sea water.
ماء النهر (ma' al-nahr): River water.
ماء البئر (ma' al-bi'r): Well water.
ماء ذاب من الثلج (ma' dhaba min al-thalj): Water obtained from melting snow, excluding melted salt.
البرد (al-barad): Hail, with fatha on ba and ra; melted hail water is included.
ماء العين (ma' al-'ayn): Spring water that gushes from the earth.
الإضافة للتعريف لا للتقييد (al-idafah li al-ta'rif la li al-taqyid): The construct phrase (e.g., 'water of the sea') is for definition (specifying which type) not restriction (making it a restricted concept).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the seven purifying waters?
A) River water
B) Sea water
C) Melted salt
D) Melted snow

Key Takeaway
Seven specific types of water are all equally purifying because each is considered mutlaq (absolute) water, confirmed by the principle that the idafah in their names is for definition, not restriction.

Taharah — Purification

The Sea Water Dispute: Hanafi Position and Refutation of Opposing View
Addressing the dispute over permissibility of sea water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the permissibility of using sea water for purification and evaluate the counter-argument from the hadith of Ibn Umar.

Core Idea
Sea water is purifying despite its saltiness; the hadith against it is weak and taken out of context.

While the seven waters are universally accepted as purifying, a historical dispute arose specifically over sea water. Some Companions, including Ibn Umar and Abu Hurairah, considered it unsuitable based on a hadith about sea travel. This lesson examines that dispute, the Hanafi response, and why the Hanafi position prevails as the mufta bihi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of the dispute
Some early authorities, including Ibn Umar and Abu Hurairah, disliked using sea water for purification because of its saltiness and odor, and a hadith they cited.
The hadith: 'None should sail the sea except a pilgrim, an Umrah performer, or a warrior in the path of Allah…'
2
Analyze the opposing evidence
The hadith is narrated only by Abu Dawud and its apparent meaning is about sea travel, not purification. The text itself is not explicit about purification.
3
Apply Hanafi hadith criticism
The Hanafi school considers the hadith weak (gharib) due to its single chain, and its context (sea travel) does not support a ruling on purification. Moreover, the saltiness does not change the water's legal status as mutlaq.
4
Conclude with the Hanafi ruling
The Hanafi position is that sea water is purifying and permissible for wudhu and ghusl. This is the mufta bihi, confirmed by ahl al-tarjih, and it accords with the general principle that any mutlaq water is purifying.
The practical consequence: Hanafis use sea water without any dislike.

Source Text

المياه التي يجوز التطهير بها سبعة مياه : ماء السماء وماء البحر، وماء النهر، وماء البئر، وماء ذاب من الثلج والبرد، وماء العين. وماء البحر التنصيص عليه دفعاً لمظنة توهم عدم جواز التطهير به؛ لأنه مرّ منتن كما توهم ذلك بعض الصحابة، ومن الناس من كره الوضوء من البحر الملح؛ لحديث ابن عمر ه: أنه قال: "لا يركب البحر إلا حاج أو معتمر أو غاز في سبيل الله؛ فإن تحت البحر ناراً، وتحت النار بحراً" تفرد به أبو داود، وكان ابن عمر لا يرى جواز الوضوء به، ولا الغسل عن جنابة، وكذا روي عن أبي هريرة. وماء النهر: قد يحرك، وهو مجرى الماء. ذاب من الثلج احترز به عن الذي يذوب من الملح؛ لأنه لا يطهر؛ يذوب في الشتاء ويجمد في الصيف عكس الماء. والبرد: بفتح الباء الموحدة والراء المهملة ماء العين [وهو الذي يتفجر من الأرض لازدياده في داخلها] اعلم أن الإضافة في هذه المياه للتعريف لا للتقييد والفرق بين الإضافتين صحة إطلاق الماء على الأول دون الثاني؛ إذ يصح بأن يقال لماء الورد: "هذا ماء من غير قيد بالورد بخلاف ماء البير؛ لصحة إطلاقه فيه.
The waters with which purification is permissible are seven waters: rainwater, sea water, river water, well water, water melted from snow and hail, and spring water. And [the mention of] sea water is specified to repel the suspicion of it not being permissible to purify with it, because it is salty and foul-smelling, as some Companions thought. And among people, there are those who disliked performing ablution with salty sea water due to the hadith of Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said: 'None should sail the sea except a pilgrim, an Umrah performer, or a warrior in the path of Allah, for under the sea is fire, and under the fire is a sea.' It was narrated exclusively by Abu Dawud. Ibn Umar did not see the permissibility of performing ablution with it, nor bathing from janabah, and similarly it is narrated from Abu Hurairah. River water: it may be pronounced with a vowel movement [nahar] and it is the course of water. [Melted from] snow is a distinction from that which melts from salt, because it [salt] does not purify; it melts in winter and freezes in summer, opposite to water. Hail: with a fatha on the ba and ra'. Spring water: it is that which gushes from the earth due to its increase inside it. Know that the idafah (construct phrase) in these [terms] is for definition (ta'rif) not restriction (taqyid). The difference between the two idafahs is the validity of applying [the word] 'water' to the first without [the second]; for example, one can say of rosewater 'this is water' without the qualifier 'rose', unlike well water, because it is valid to call it water without restriction.

Commentary
This passage presents the classic Hanafi ruling on the seven waters that are legally pure and purifying (mutahhir). The list includes: rain, sea, river, well, melted snow/hail, and spring water. The author immediately addresses a potential doubt about sea water: despite its saltiness and odor, some early authorities (like Ibn Umar and Abu Hurairah) considered it unsuitable. The commentary quotes a hadith (though weak, as only Abu Dawud narrated it) used by those who disliked sea water. However, the Hanafi position (following the majority of scholars) is that sea water is perfectly permissible for purification, and the mention here confirms that. The author clarifies that 'melted from snow' excludes salt, as salt water is not purifying. He explains the grammar: the idafah (construct) in these terms is for definition (like 'water of the sea' simply identifies the type) not restriction (like 'rosewater', which restricts the meaning). Key Terms
تفرد به أبو داود (tafarrada bihi Abu Dawud): The hadith was narrated exclusively by Abu Dawud, meaning no other compiler recorded it, indicating weakness (gharib).
مظنة توهم (mazinnat tawahhum): A plausible suspicion or misconception that could arise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Sea water is purifying and permissible for wudhu and ghusl in the Hanafi school, despite the weak opposing hadith. The school's tarjih is based on hadith criticism and the principle that mutlaq water remains purifying regardless of saltiness.

Taharah — Purification
beginner
8 min
The Seven Purifying Waters
Lists seven purifying waters and defines key terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the seven purifying waters and explain the Hanafi criterion for ma' mutlaq.

Core Idea
Seven waters purify: rain, sea, river, well, snow, hail, spring; all are mutlaq unless nature changes.

This lesson opens Kitab al-Tahara by listing the seven waters valid for purification. You will learn the basic classification and understand why even seawater, despite its saltiness, is purifying.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the seven waters
Read the list: rainwater, seawater, river water, well water, melted snow, hail, spring water. Repeat until committed.
Each is considered mutlaq unless changed in nature.
2
Understand 'yajuz' as validity
The author clarifies that 'yajuz' here means the water is valid (sahih) for purification, not necessarily permissible in all contexts (e.g., without owner's permission).
This is a key Hanafi precision.
3
Note the idafa distinction
The construct phrase in 'well water' is for definition, not restriction; unlike 'rosewater', 'well water' retains the absolute name 'water'.
This ensures all seven are considered mutlaq water.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة
المياه التي يجوز التطهير بها سبعة مياه : ماء السماء وماء البحر، وماء النهر، وماء البئر، وماء ذاب من الثلج والبرد، وماء العين.
كتاب: الكتاب والكتابة لغة: الجمع، وأطلق الكتابة على هذه النقوش؛ لما فيه من جمع حروفها بعضها إلى بعض، واصطلاحاً طائفة من المسائل الفقهية اعتبرت مستقلة، شملت أنواعاً كهذا الكتاب؛ فإن فيه طهارة الوضوء، وطهارة الغسل، والطهارة بالماء، والطهارة بالتراب إلى غير ذلك، أو لم تشمل بأن لم يكن تحته باب ولا فصل ككتاب اللقطة واللقيط والآبق والمفقود، وإنما زدنا قولنا: "اعتبرت"؛ ليدخل نحو الطهارة، فإنها من توابع الصلاة إلا إنها اعتبرت مستقلة، أي اعتبرها المعبر به مستقلة بحيث لا يتوقف تصور ما فيه على شيء قبله أو بعده. [حاشية الطحطاوي على مراقي الفلاح: ١٨]
الطهارة بفتح الفاء أفصح من ضمها (حاشية الطحطاوي) قدمت الطهارة على الصلاة؛ لكونها شرطاً وهو مقدم مراقي الفلاح: ۱۸]
الطهارة بفتح الطاء مصدر طهر الشيء بمعنى النظافة، وبكسرها الآلة كالماء والتراب، وبضمها اسم لما فضل بعد التطهير [مراقي الفلاح: ۱۸]
يجوز: أراد بالجواز الصحة؛ لئلا يرد ما يرد على ظاهر العبارة من أن الماء المملوك للغير - كما إذا أحرزه في جبّ وغيره - إذا توضأ غير المالك به لا يجوز، أي لا يحل به الوضوء، ولكنه يصح أي يترتب عليه صحة الصلاة.
وماء البحر التنصيص عليه دفعاً لمظنة توهم عدم جواز التطهير به؛ لأنه مرّ منتن كما توهم ذلك بعض الصحابة، ومن الناس من كره الوضوء من البحر الملح؛ لحديث ابن عمر ه: أنه قال: "لا يركب البحر إلا حاج أو معتمر أو غاز في سبيل الله؛ فإن تحت البحر ناراً، وتحت النار بحراً تفرد به أبو داود، وكان ابن عمر لا يرى جواز الوضوء به، ولا الغسل عن جنابة، وكذا روي عن أبي هريرة. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٢٠]
وماء النهر : قد يحرك ، وهو مجرى الماء.
ذاب من الثلج احترز به عن الذي يذوب من الملح؛ لأنه لا يطهر؛ يذوب في الشتاء ويجمد في الصيف عكس الماء. [مراقي الفلاح: ٢١]
والبرد: بفتح الباء الموحدة والراء المهملة ماء العين [وهو الذي يتفجر من الأرض لازدياده في داخلها]
اعلم أن الإضافة في هذه المياه للتعريف لا للتقييد والفرق بين الإضافتين صحة إطلاق الماء على الأول دون الثاني؛ إذ يصح بأن يقال لماء الورد: "هذا ماء من غير قيد بالورد بخلاف ماء البير؛ لصحة إطلاقه فيه.
لا
The Book of Purification. The waters by which purification is permissible are seven waters: rainwater, seawater, river water, well water, water melted from snow and hail, and spring water. Kitab: lexically it means collection, and it is applied to these writings because they involve collecting letters together; technically it is a group of independent fiqh issues that encompass types like this book, for it contains the purification of wudu, the purification of ghusl, purification with water, purification with dust, etc., or does not encompass if there is no chapter or section under it like the book of lost property, foundling, runaway slave, and missing person. We added the word 'considered' to include such as tahara, for it is a subsidiary of prayer but is considered independent, meaning the author considered it independent such that understanding its content does not depend on anything before or after it. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi]. Tahara: with fatha on ta is more eloquent than with damma. Tahara is placed before prayer because it is a condition, and a condition precedes. Tahara with fatha on ta is the masdar meaning cleanliness, with kasra it means the instrument like water and dust, with damma it means what remains after purification. 'Yajuz' (is permissible): by permissibility he means validity; to avoid the objection that water owned by another—if one performs wudu with it without the owner's permission—it is not permissible (halal) but it is valid (sahih), meaning the prayer is sound. Mentioning seawater specifically removes the false notion that purification with it is not permissible because it is salty and foul-smelling, as some Companions thought. Some people disliked wudu with seawater due to the hadith of Ibn Umar: 'None should sail the sea except a pilgrim, one performing umra, or a warrior in the path of Allah; for under the sea is fire and under the fire is sea' (reported only by Abu Dawud). Ibn Umar did not consider it permissible for wudu or ghusl from janaba; similarly narrated from Abu Huraira. River water: may be vowelized, meaning the course of water. Melted from snow: he excludes what melts from salt, for it does not purify; it melts in winter and freezes in summer, opposite of water. Hail: with fatha on ba and ra, spring water: which gushes from the earth due to its abundance within. Know that the idafa (construct phrase) in these waters is for definition, not restriction; the difference between the two idafas is the validity of applying the word 'water' to the first without the second, for one can say of rosewater 'this is water' without restriction, unlike well water because it can be called water without restriction. No.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is the opening of Kitab al-Tahara in Nur al-Idah. It begins by listing the seven types of water that are valid for purification: rainwater, seawater, river water, well water, water melted from snow, hail, and spring water. The commentary then defines the term 'kitab' linguistically and technically, explaining that it refers to a self-contained collection of fiqh issues that do not depend on prior or subsequent knowledge. It clarifies that tahara (purification) precedes prayer because it is a condition. The author distinguishes the vowels of tahara: fatha for the act, kasra for the instrument, damma for the leftover water. The explanation of 'yajuz' indicates it refers to validity (sihha), not permissibility (hilla), so wudu with another's water without permission is valid but not halal. Seawater is specifically mentioned to counter the misunderstanding that it is not purifying due to its saltiness; the hadith of Ibn Umar is cited but noted as weak. The commentary on melted water excludes salt water. The idafa (construct phrase) in the names of waters is for definition, not restriction—so 'well water' is not like 'rosewater' because 'well water' can still be called 'water' absolutely.

Key Terms
الطهارة (al-taharah): Spiritual cleanliness that is a condition for prayer, performed with water or earth.
يجوز (yajuz): Here it means validity (sihha), not permissibility (ibaha) in the sense of being lawful without sin.
ماء مطلق (ma' mutlaq): Water that has not been mixed with anything that makes it restricted (muqayyad); the default purifying water.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many types of purifying water are listed in this passage?
A) Five
B) Seven
C) Three
D) Nine

Key Takeaway
Seven types of water are purifying; as long as water retains its absolute name, it is valid for purification.

Taharah — Purification
beginner

Key Definitions: Kitab, Tahara, and Yajuz
Defines key terms: kitab, tahara, yajuz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'kitab' in fiqh context, distinguish the three meanings of tahara based on vowelization, and explain why 'yajuz' is interpreted as validity.

Core Idea
Kitab means a self-contained collection; tahara has three forms; 'yajuz' in fiqh often signals validity, not permissibility.

This lesson unpacks the technical definitions that underpin Kitab al-Tahara. Understanding these terms is crucial for reading any Hanafi fiqh text accurately.

Guided Path
1
Define 'kitab' lexically and technically
Lexically, kitab means collection. Technically in fiqh, it refers to a self-contained collection of independent rulings that do not depend on prior chapters.
The author adds 'considered' to include topics like tahara that are technically subsidiary but treated independently.
2
Learn the three vowel patterns of 'tahara'
With fatha on ta: the act of purification. With kasra: the instrument (water, dust). With damma: the leftover water after purification.
The most eloquent pronunciation is with fatha.
3
Understand 'yajuz' as validity (sihha)
The author clarifies that 'yajuz' here means validity, not permissibility. This avoids the contradiction that wudu with another's water without permission is valid but not halal.
This precision prevents incorrect inference that validity and permissibility are identical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة
المياه التي يجوز التطهير بها سبعة مياه : ماء السماء وماء البحر، وماء النهر، وماء البئر، وماء ذاب من الثلج والبرد، وماء العين.
كتاب: الكتاب والكتابة لغة: الجمع، وأطلق الكتابة على هذه النقوش؛ لما فيه من جمع حروفها بعضها إلى بعض، واصطلاحاً طائفة من المسائل الفقهية اعتبرت مستقلة، شملت أنواعاً كهذا الكتاب؛ فإن فيه طهارة الوضوء، وطهارة الغسل، والطهارة بالماء، والطهارة بالتراب إلى غير ذلك، أو لم تشمل بأن لم يكن تحته باب ولا فصل ككتاب اللقطة واللقيط والآبق والمفقود، وإنما زدنا قولنا: "اعتبرت"؛ ليدخل نحو الطهارة، فإنها من توابع الصلاة إلا إنها اعتبرت مستقلة، أي اعتبرها المعبر به مستقلة بحيث لا يتوقف تصور ما فيه على شيء قبله أو بعده. [حاشية الطحطاوي على مراقي الفلاح: ١٨]
الطهارة بفتح الفاء أفصح من ضمها (حاشية الطحطاوي) قدمت الطهارة على الصلاة؛ لكونها شرطاً وهو مقدم مراقي الفلاح: ۱۸]
الطهارة بفتح الطاء مصدر طهر الشيء بمعنى النظافة، وبكسرها الآلة كالماء والتراب، وبضمها اسم لما فضل بعد التطهير [مراقي الفلاح: ۱۸]
يجوز: أراد بالجواز الصحة؛ لئلا يرد ما يرد على ظاهر العبارة من أن الماء المملوك للغير - كما إذا أحرزه في جبّ وغيره - إذا توضأ غير المالك به لا يجوز، أي لا يحل به الوضوء، ولكنه يصح أي يترتب عليه صحة الصلاة.
وماء البحر التنصيص عليه دفعاً لمظنة توهم عدم جواز التطهير به؛ لأنه مرّ منتن كما توهم ذلك بعض الصحابة، ومن الناس من كره الوضوء من البحر الملح؛ لحديث ابن عمر ه: أنه قال: "لا يركب البحر إلا حاج أو معتمر أو غاز في سبيل الله؛ فإن تحت البحر ناراً، وتحت النار بحراً تفرد به أبو داود، وكان ابن عمر لا يرى جواز الوضوء به، ولا الغسل عن جنابة، وكذا روي عن أبي هريرة. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٢٠]
وماء النهر : قد يحرك ، وهو مجرى الماء.
ذاب من الثلج احترز به عن الذي يذوب من الملح؛ لأنه لا يطهر؛ يذوب في الشتاء ويجمد في الصيف عكس الماء. [مراقي الفلاح: ٢١]
والبرد: بفتح الباء الموحدة والراء المهملة ماء العين [وهو الذي يتفجر من الأرض لازدياده في داخلها]
اعلم أن الإضافة في هذه المياه للتعريف لا للتقييد والفرق بين الإضافتين صحة إطلاق الماء على الأول دون الثاني؛ إذ يصح بأن يقال لماء الورد: "هذا ماء من غير قيد بالورد بخلاف ماء البير؛ لصحة إطلاقه فيه.
لا
The Book of Purification. The waters by which purification is permissible are seven waters: rainwater, seawater, river water, well water, water melted from snow and hail, and spring water. Kitab: lexically it means collection, and it is applied to these writings because they involve collecting letters together; technically it is a group of independent fiqh issues that encompass types like this book, for it contains the purification of wudu, the purification of ghusl, purification with water, purification with dust, etc., or does not encompass if there is no chapter or section under it like the book of lost property, foundling, runaway slave, and missing person. We added the word 'considered' to include such as tahara, for it is a subsidiary of prayer but is considered independent, meaning the author considered it independent such that understanding its content does not depend on anything before or after it. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi]. Tahara: with fatha on ta is more eloquent than with damma. Tahara is placed before prayer because it is a condition, and a condition precedes. Tahara with fatha on ta is the masdar meaning cleanliness, with kasra it means the instrument like water and dust, with damma it means what remains after purification. 'Yajuz' (is permissible): by permissibility he means validity; to avoid the objection that water owned by another—if one performs wudu with it without the owner's permission—it is not permissible (halal) but it is valid (sahih), meaning the prayer is sound. Mentioning seawater specifically removes the false notion that purification with it is not permissible because it is salty and foul-smelling, as some Companions thought. Some people disliked wudu with seawater due to the hadith of Ibn Umar: 'None should sail the sea except a pilgrim, one performing umra, or a warrior in the path of Allah; for under the sea is fire and under the fire is sea' (reported only by Abu Dawud). Ibn Umar did not consider it permissible for wudu or ghusl from janaba; similarly narrated from Abu Huraira. River water: may be vowelized, meaning the course of water. Melted from snow: he excludes what melts from salt, for it does not purify; it melts in winter and freezes in summer, opposite of water. Hail: with fatha on ba and ra, spring water: which gushes from the earth due to its abundance within. Know that the idafa (construct phrase) in these waters is for definition, not restriction; the difference between the two idafas is the validity of applying the word 'water' to the first without the second, for one can say of rosewater 'this is water' without restriction, unlike well water because it can be called water without restriction. No.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is the opening of Kitab al-Tahara in Nur al-Idah. It begins by listing the seven types of water that are valid for purification: rainwater, seawater, river water, well water, water melted from snow, hail, and spring water. The commentary then defines the term 'kitab' linguistically and technically, explaining that it refers to a self-contained collection of fiqh issues that do not depend on prior or subsequent knowledge. It clarifies that tahara (purification) precedes prayer because it is a condition. The author distinguishes the vowels of tahara: fatha for the act, kasra for the instrument, damma for the leftover water. The explanation of 'yajuz' indicates it refers to validity (sihha), not permissibility (hilla), so wudu with another's water without permission is valid but not halal. Seawater is specifically mentioned to counter the misunderstanding that it is not purifying due to its saltiness; the hadith of Ibn Umar is cited but noted as weak. The commentary on melted water excludes salt water. The idafa (construct phrase) in the names of waters is for definition, not restriction—so 'well water' is not like 'rosewater' because 'well water' can still be called 'water' absolutely.

Key Terms
الطهارة (al-taharah): Spiritual cleanliness that is a condition for prayer, performed with water or earth.
يجوز (yajuz): Here it means validity (sihha), not permissibility (ibaha) in the sense of being lawful without sin.
كتاب (kitab): Technically, a group of independent fiqh issues considered self-contained.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does 'yajuz' mean according to the author in this passage?
A) Permissible (halal)
B) Valid (sahih)
C) Recommended
D) Obligatory

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh terminology, 'yajuz' often signals validity (sihha), not mere permissibility; understanding this prevents misapplication.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
The Five Categories of Water: Purifying and Non-Purifying
Categorizes water into five types with rulings on purity and permissibility for purification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three main water categories described, state their ruling (tahir/mutahhir/makruh), and distinguish musta'mal from absolute water.

Core Idea
Absolute water is the only fully purifying type; musta'mal is pure but non-purifying for hadath.

Water is the primary purifier in Islamic law, but not all water is equally valid for ritual purification. This passage from Nur al-Idah and its commentary divides water into five categories, three of which are detailed here, with important distinctions between purity (taharah) and purifying ability (tuhuriyyah). Understanding these categories is essential for valid wudu and ghusl.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five water categories
List the five types as given in the matn. Focus on the three described in detail: absolute water, water drunk by a cat (similar animals), and musta'mal water. Note that the remaining two (water from trees/fruits and water changed by cooking) are only briefly mentioned.
The full five are: tahir mutahhir ghayr makruh, tahir mutahhir makruh, tahir ghayr mutahhir, water from trees/fruits (not valid), water changed by cooking (not valid).
2
Distinguish absolute water from musta'mal
Absolute water (ma' mutlaq) is pure and purifying, unchanged by mixture. Musta'mal is water that has been used to remove hadath or for a devotional act; it remains pure (tahir) but loses its ability to purify for hadath (ghayr mutahhir).
Musta'mal can still be used for cleaning impure objects (najasa) but not for wudu/ghusl.
3
Understand the ruling on cat's leftover water
Water drunk by a domestic cat in a small quantity is pure and purifying but makruh (disliked). The amount is determined by the observer's view. For wild cats, the leftover water is najis.
"Little" is what the observer would consider little; detailed measures come later in the text.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on musta'mal
The author holds water becomes musta'mal immediately upon separation from the body. Al-Tahawi and some Balkh scholars hold it becomes musta'mal only after settling. The practical difference: if the used water falls on another limb without settling, the author says washing with it is invalid; al-Tahawi says it is valid.
The mufta bihi is the author's view (immediate separation).

Source Text

أقسام المياه
ثم المياه على خمسة أقسام طاهر مطهر غير مكروه: وهو الماء المطلق. الثاني: طاهر مطهر مكروه وهو ما شربت منه الهرة ونحوها وكان قليلا. الثالث: طاهر غير مطهر: وهو ما استعمل لرفع حدث أو لقربة كالوضوء على الوضوء بنيته، ويصير الماء مستعملا بمجرد انفصاله عن الجسد، ولا يجوز بماء شجر وثمر ولو خرج غير عصر في الأظهر، ولا بماء زال طبعه بالطبخ أو بغلبة غيره عليه.
الماء المطلق وهو الذي لم يخالطه ما يصير به مقيدا. الهرة: أي الأهلية؛ إذ الوحشية سؤرها نجس [مراقي الفلاح: ٢٢] قليلا ومقدار القليل والكثير سيأتي بالتفصيل] ظاهر المذهب أنه ما يعدّه الناظر قليلا. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٢٢] غير مطهر : للحدث لا للنجاسة الحقيقية. وهو: اعلم أن هذا على سبيل منع الخلو؛ فإنه إذا توضأ المحدث ونوى الوضوء يرتفع الحدث ويثاب المتوضئ، وإذا توضأ غير المحدث ونوى الوضوء مع اختلاف المجلسين وأداء عبادة توضأ لها لا يرتفع الحدث؛ لأن ارتفاع الحدث فرع ثبوته ولكن يثاب المتوضئ للنية ، وإذا توضأ المحدث ولم ينو الوضوء يرتفع الحدث ولا يثاب، وفي هذه الصور الثلاث يكون الماء مستعملاً، أما إذا توضأ غير المحدث و لم ينو الوضوء لا يكون الماء مستعملا لانتفاء الأمرين. أو لقربة هي فعل ما يثاب عليه، ولا ثواب إلا بالنية. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ۲۲] كالوضوء إلخ: أطلقه الشيخ، وهو مقيد باختلاف المجلس؛ فإنه إذا اتحد المجلسان يكره الوضوء الثاني، ولا يكون الماء الثاني مستعملاً، إذ لم يؤد بالأول عبادة شرع التطهير لها، وإلا فلا يكره. ويصير إلخ: أي يصير الماء مستعملاً؛ وقت زواله عن العضو وقت الاستعمال من غير توقف، واختار الطحاوي وبعض مشايخ بلخ أنه لا يستعمل إلا إذا استقر، وتظهر فائدة الخلاف فيما إذا انفصل و لم يستقر فسقط على عضو آخر، وجرى عليه من غير أن يأخذه بيده فعلى ما قاله الشيخ لا يصح غسل ذلك العضو بذلك الماء، وعلى قول الطحاوي مظله حاشية الطحطاوي: ٢٣] يصح. شجر المراد به مطلق النبات في الأظهر احترز به عما قيل بأنه يجوز بما يقطر بنفسه؛ لأنه ليس لخروجه بلا عصر تأثير في نفي القيد، وصحة نفي الاسم عنه. مراقي الفلاح: ٢٤] طبعه وهو الرقة والسيلان والإرواء والإنبات. بالطبخ: قيد به؛ لأنه لو تغير وصف الماء بنحو الحمص، أو الباقلا، بدون طبخ بأن ألقي فيه ليبتل، و لم تذهب رقة الماء، فإنه يجوز التوضؤ به حاشية الطحطاوي: ٢٤]
Divisions of Waters. Then the waters are of five categories: 1) Pure and purifying, not makruh: that is absolute water. 2) Pure and purifying but makruh: that from which a cat or similar has drunk and it is little. 3) Pure but not purifying: that which has been used to remove hadath or for an act of worship, like wudu performed over wudu with intention, and water becomes used (musta'mal) simply by separating from the body, and it is not permissible to perform wudu with water from trees and fruits even if it comes out without pressing in the apparent view, nor with water whose nature has been removed by cooking or by something else overwhelming it. Absolute water is that which has not been mixed with something that makes it restricted. The cat: meaning domestic; for the wild cat, its leftover water is najis. Little: the measure of little and much will come in detail; the apparent madhhab is that it is what the observer considers little. Not purifying: meaning for hadath, not for actual impurity (najasa). It is: know that this is to prevent emptiness; for if a person with hadath performs wudu and intends wudu, the hadath is removed and he is rewarded; if one without hadath performs wudu intending wudu with differing assemblies and performing worship for which he performs wudu, hadath is not removed because its removal depends on its existence, but he is rewarded for the intention; if a person with hadath performs wudu without intending wudu, the hadath is removed but he is not rewarded; in these three cases the water becomes musta'mal. If one without hadath performs wudu without intention, the water does not become musta'mal because both conditions are absent. Or for an act of worship: it is doing something for which one is rewarded, and there is no reward except with intention. Like wudu etc.: the author gave it absolutely, but it is restricted by differing assemblies; for if the two assemblies are the same, the second wudu is makruh and the water does not become musta'mal, since the first did not fulfill an act of worship for which purification was prescribed; otherwise it is not makruh. And it becomes: meaning water becomes musta'mal at the moment it leaves the limb at the time of use without delay; al-Tahawi and some scholars of Balkh chose that it does not become musta'mal until it settles. The benefit of the difference appears when water separates and does not settle, falling on another limb and flowing over it without being taken by hand: according to the author, washing that limb with that water is not valid; according to al-Tahawi, it is valid. Tree: meaning absolute plants in the apparent view; he excludes what is said that it is permissible with that which drips by itself, because its coming out without pressing has no effect on negating the restriction and the validity of negating the name from it. Its nature: which is fluidity, flow, quenching, and growth. By cooking: he restricts it; for if the description of water changes by something like chickpeas or broad beans without cooking, by being thrown in to soak, and the fluidity of the water does not disappear, then it is permissible to perform wudu with it.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage categorizes water into five types, though only three are detailed here. The first category is absolute water (ma' mutlaq) which is pure and purifying without any dislike. The second is water drunk by a domestic cat (or similar animal) in a small quantity; it remains pure and purifying but is makruh (disliked). The third is used water (musta'mal): water that has been used to remove ritual impurity (hadath) or for a devotional act, like performing wudu while already in wudu with intention. Such water is pure in itself but cannot be used to remove hadath again. The commentary clarifies that water becomes musta'mal as soon as it separates from the body, according to the author, though al-Tahawi holds it becomes musta'mal only after it settles. The difference matters if the water falls on another limb: the author says it is not valid to wash with it, al-Tahawi says it is. The passage also prohibits using water from trees and fruits (like sap) even if it comes out without pressing, because it is not absolute water. Similarly, water whose nature (fluidity, etc.) has been changed by cooking or by another substance overwhelming it is not valid for purification. However, if water changes due to soaking legumes without cooking, it remains valid as long as its fluidity remains. The discussion of intention (niyya) is tied to whether water becomes used: if one without hadath performs wudu without intention, the water does not become musta'mal.

Key Terms
مستعمل (musta'mal): Water that has been used for removing hadath or for a devotional act; it is pure but cannot be used again for purification from hadath.
ماء مطلق (ma' mutlaq): Water in its original state, not mixed with anything that restricts it or changes its nature.
سؤر الهرة (su'r al-hirra): The leftover water from which a domestic cat has drunk.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Only after settling
B) Immediately upon separating from the body
C) After being left for one hour
D) When it is mixed with another substance

Key Takeaway
Absolute water is the only fully purifying water; musta'mal water is pure but not for hadath; cat's leftover water is pure but disliked if little.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
15 min
Divisions of Water and the Ruling on Water Overcome by Pure Substances
Explains the five categories of water and the issue of ghalabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five categories of water in Hanafi fiqh, explain when water becomes musta'mal, and apply the criteria for ghalabah to determine the validity of wudu.

Core Idea
Water retains its purifying quality unless overcome by a liquid with distinct attributes or rendered non-fluid by a solid.

This passage opens the Book of Purification by classifying water into five categories, a foundational step for all acts of worship involving water. The detailed rules for 'used water' (musta'mal) and 'overcome water' (ghalabah) are essential for determining which waters are valid for wudu and ghusl. The text also presents an intra-madhhab dispute on when water becomes musta'mal, highlighting the precision of Hanafi legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five water categories
List each of the five divisions: mutlaq (absolutely pure), disliked pure, pure but not purifying (musta'mal), impure (najis), and another pure but disliked (from cat/similar). Understand the defining characteristic of each.
Focus on the first four as explicitly named; the fifth is implied in the commentary.
2
Understand the concept of musta'mal water
Define musta'mal water as water used for removing hadath or perform a qurbah. Note that it is tahir (pure) but not mutahir (purifying) for hadath. Recognize the role of intention and the moment it becomes used.
The text states it becomes used 'simply by its separation from the body.'
3
Analyze the intra-madhhab dispute on musta'mal water
Compare two positions: Abu Hanifa's view (water becomes used upon separation) vs. al-Tahawi's view (water becomes used only after settling). Understand the practical consequence: can water that drips onto another limb be used to wash that limb?
The tarjih (preferred view) is Abu Hanifa's, as per the commentary.
4
Learn the rules for ghalabah (overcome water)
Distinguish between solids and liquids. For solids: water is valid as long as it remains fluid (thin and flowing). For liquids: check the number of attributes (color, taste, smell). If liquid has 1 attribute, appearing that attribute suffices to invalidate; if 2 attributes, appearance of 1 invalidates; if 3 attributes, appearance of 2 invalidates. For liquids with no attributes (e.g., used water), use weight criterion.
Change by a solid (e.g., saffron) does not harm unless water loses fluidity.
5
Apply the rules to determine water validity
Given a scenario, classify the water and state whether wudu is valid. Example: 2 ratls used water mixed with 1 ratl mutlaq water → wudu invalid. Vice versa → valid. Equal mixing → precaution is to treat as overcome.
The fourth category (impure water) is defined separately by the presence of impurity.

الماء المطلق وهو الذي لم يخالطه ما يصير به مقيدا. الهرة: أي الأهلية؛ إذ الوحشية سؤرها نجس [مراقي الفلاح: ٢٢] قليلا ومقدار القليل والكثير سيأتي بالتفصيل] ظاهر المذهب أنه ما يعدّه الناظر قليلا. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٢٢] غير مطهر : للحدث لا للنجاسة الحقيقية. وهو: اعلم أن هذا على سبيل منع الخلو؛ فإنه إذا توضأ المحدث ونوى الوضوء يرتفع الحدث ويثاب المتوضئ، وإذا توضأ غير المحدث ونوى الوضوء مع اختلاف المجلسين وأداء عبادة توضاً لها لا يرتفع الحدث؛ لأن ارتفاع الحدث فرع ثبوته ولكن يثاب المتوضئ للنية ، وإذا توضأ المحدث ولم ينو الوضوء يرتفع الحدث ولا يثاب، وفي هذه الصور الثلاث يكون الماء مستعملاً، أما إذا توضأ غير المحدث و لم ينو الوضوء لا يكون الماء مستعملا لانتفاء الأمرين. أو لقربة هي فعل ما يثاب عليه، ولا ثواب إلا بالنية. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ۲۲] كالوضوء إلخ: أطلقه الشيخ، وهو مقيد باختلاف المجلس؛ فإنه إذا اتحد المجلسان يكره الوضوء الثاني، ولا يكون الماء الثاني مستعملاً، إذ لم يؤد بالأول عبادة شرع التطهير لها، وإلا فلا يكره. ويصير إلخ: أي يصير الماء مستعملاً؛ وقت زواله عن العضو وقت الاستعمال من غير توقف، واختار الطحاوي وبعض مشايخ بلخ أنه لا يستعمل إلا إذا استقر، وتظهر فائدة الخلاف فيما إذا انفصل و لم يستقر فسقط على عضو آخر، وجرى عليه من غير أن يأخذه بيده فعلى ما قاله الشيخ لا يصح غسل ذلك العضو بذلك الماء، وعلى قول الطحاوي مظله حاشية الطحطاوي: ٢٣] يصح. شجر المراد به مطلق النبات في الأظهر احترز به عما قيل بأنه يجوز بما يقطر بنفسه؛ لأنه ليس لخروجه بلا عصر تأثير في نفي" القيد، وصحة نفي الاسم عنه. مراقي الفلاح: ٢٤] طبعه وهو الرقة والسيلان والإرواء والإنبات. بالطبخ: قيد به؛ لأنه لو تغير وصف الماء بنحو الحمص، أو الباقلا، بدون طبخ بأن ألقي فيه ليبتل، و لم تذهب
رقة الماء، فإنه يجوز التوضؤ به حاشية الطحطاوي: ٢٤]

والغلبة في مخالطة الجامدات بإخراج الماء عن رقته وسيلانه، ولا يضر تغير أوصافه كلها بجامد كزعفران وفاكهة وورق شجر.

القسم الرابع من المياه
الغلبة الماء المقيد
والغلبة: شروع في تفصيل الغلبة في صورة الضابطة، فإن الغلبة مختلفة باختلاف المخالط بغير طبخ رقته حتى أنه لا يتسرى من الثوب سيلانه بأن لا يسيل على الأعضاء مثل سيلان الماء. ولا يضر أي لا يمنع جواز الوضوء به كلها لو خالطه شيء طاهر بدون طبخ بجامد جمد الماء وكل سائل جمودا بمعنى صلب وغلظ ضد ذاب. فاكهة كصاحبة، جميع الفواكه، لا التمر والعنب والرمان فقط وصف واحد مثل اللون فقط، أو الطعم. فقط : ولا يوجد له وصف ثالث. كاللبن: فإن لم يوجدا جاز به الوضوء، وإن وجد أحدهما لم يجز، كما لو كان المخالط له وصف واحد فظهر وصفه، كبعض البطيخ والقرع فإن ماءهما لا يخالف إلا في الطعم، وكماء الورد فإنه لا يخالف إلا في الريح. (مراقي الفلاح وحاشية (الطحطاوي كالخل له لون وطعم وريح، فأيّ وصفين منها ظهرا منعا صحة الوضوء، والواحد منها لا يضر لقلته مراقي الفلاح: ٢٦] كالماء المستعمل : فإنه بالاستعمال لم يتغير له طعم ولا لون ريح وإنما اعتبرنا الغلبة بالوزن ههنا؛ لعدم التمييز بالوصف لفقده. (مراقي الفلاح بتصرف) الماء المستعمل أو ماء الورد الذي انقطعت رائحته وبعكسه وهو ما لو كان رطلان من الماء المطلق، ورطل الماء المستعمل أو ماء الورد المنقطع الرائحة جاز به الوضوء، وإن استوى الماء المطلق والمقيد لم يذكر حكمه من في ظاهر الرواية، وقال المشايخ حكمه حكم المغلوب احتياطاً. (مراقي الفلاح) نجاسة: [وعلم وقوعها يقينا أو بغلبة الظن أطلقها الشيخ وهي مقيدة بغير قليل الأرواث؛ فإن نجاسة الماء بوقوع
ولا
:
النجاسة فيه محله في غير قليل الأرواث إذا وقع في الآبار. (حاشية الطحطاوي)
The Book of Purification. 1. Divisions of Waters. Then waters are of five divisions: pure and purifying not disliked: that is the absolute water; it is pure in itself and purifying for others for hadath and janabah. Second: pure and purifying disliked: that from which the cat and its like drink while it is little. Third: pure not purifying: like birds of prey. Fourth: that which is used for removing hadath or for a drawing near (qurbah), like wudu upon wudu with intention; and the water becomes used simply by its separation from the body; and it is not permissible with water of tree and fruit even if it comes out without pressing in the apparent view, nor with water whose nature has changed by cooking or by being overcome by another. Explanation of terms: absolute water: that which nothing has mixed with it to make it restricted. Cat: meaning domestic; for the wild one its remnant is impure. Little: its measure will come later. The apparent madhhab is that it is what the observer considers little. Not purifying: for hadath, not for real impurity. Know that this is to prevent emptiness; for if a person in hadath performs wudu intending it, the hadath is removed and he is rewarded; if one not in hadath intends wudu with different gatherings and performing a worship for which he performs wudu, the hadath is not removed because its removal depends on existence, but he is rewarded for the intention; if a person in hadath performs wudu without intention, the hadath is removed but he is not rewarded. In these three cases the water becomes used. But if one not in hadath performs wudu without intention, the water does not become used due to absence of both matters. Or for a drawing near: that is doing something for which one is rewarded, and there is no reward except with intention. Like wudu etc: the shaykh released it; it is restricted by difference of gathering; for if the gatherings are the same, the second wudu is disliked and the water does not become used, since the first did not fulfill a worship for which purification is legislated. It becomes used: becomes used at the time of its leaving the limb at the time of use without waiting; al-Tahawi and some shuyukh of Balkh chose that it is not used until it settles. The benefit of the difference appears when it separates and does not settle and falls on another limb and flows over it without being taken by hand: according to the shaykh, washing that limb with that water is not valid; according to al-Tahawi it is valid. Tree: meaning absolute plant in the apparent view, to guard against what was said that it is permissible with that which drips by itself because its coming out without pressing has no effect in negating the restriction and the validity of negating the name. Its nature: which is thinness, flow, quenching, and growth. By cooking: restricted to it; for if the water's description changes by something like chickpeas or broad beans without cooking by being thrown in to soak and the water's thinness does not disappear, then it is permissible to perform wudu with it. And being overcome in mixing with solids by removing water from its thinness and flow; and change of all its descriptions by a solid like saffron, fruit, and tree leaves does not harm. And being overcome in liquids by appearance of one description from a liquid that has only two descriptions, like milk: it has color and taste and no smell, and by appearance of two descriptions from a liquid that has three like vinegar. And being overcome in a liquid that has no description like used water and rose water whose fragrance is cut off is by weight. If two ratls of used water mix with one ratl of absolute water, wudu is not permissible; and vice versa it is permissible. The fourth: impure water, that in which impurity has fallen. Explanation: being overcome: a start of detailing being overcome in the form of the rule; for being overcome differs according to what mixes without cooking, its thinness until it does not seep from the garment nor flow on limbs like water's flow. 'Does not harm' meaning does not prevent the permissibility of wudu with it all of it if something pure mixes without cooking with a solid that makes water solid and every liquid solidification meaning solid and thick opposite of melted. Fruit like all fruits, not only dates, grapes, and pomegranates. One description like color only or taste. 'Only': meaning it has no third description. Like milk: if neither of the two appear, wudu is permissible; if one appears, it is not. As if the mixing substance has one description and its description appears, like some watermelon and squash whose water differs only in taste, and like rose water which differs only in smell. Like vinegar: it has color, taste, and smell; whichever two of them appear prevent the validity of wudu, and one of them does not harm due to its smallness. (Marāqī al-Falāḥ and Ḥāshiyat al-Ṭaḥṭāwī) Like used water: for by use it has not changed in taste, color, or smell; rather we consider being overcome by weight here due to lack of distinction by description due to its absence. (Marāqī al-Falāḥ with modification) Used water or rose water whose fragrance has cut off, and its opposite: that if there are two ratls of absolute water and one ratl of used water or rose water with cut-off fragrance, wudu is permissible. If the absolute and restricted are equal, its ruling is not mentioned in the apparent narration, and the shaykhs said its ruling is like that of the overcome as a precaution. (Marāqī al-Falāḥ) Impurity: [And knowledge of its occurrence certainly or by overwhelming probability; the shaykh released it, and it is restricted to other than little droppings; for the impurity of water by falling of impurity in it has its place in other than little droppings when it falls in wells. (Ḥāshiyat al-Ṭaḥṭāwī)
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with commentary, opening the Book of Purification and presenting the five divisions of water according to the Hanafi school. The author first lists the categories: (1) pure and purifying without dislike (mutlaq water: rain, river, sea, well water unchanged by impurities), (2) pure and purifying but disliked (water from which a cat or similar animal has drunk, but only if it is a small quantity—since cats are domestic and their saliva is considered pure but disliked due to their habit of eating mice and the like), (3) pure but not purifying (water used for removing ritual impurity or for a devotional act, like performing wudu without having broken it; this water is valid for removing physical impurities but not for ritual purification), (4) impure water (water in which an impurity has fallen and changed one of its three properties—color, taste, smell—or even if unchanged but the impurity is heavy enough to nullify its purifying quality), and (5) pure but purifying with dislike (as per earlier commentary). The text then dives into detailed explanations: the definition of mutlaq water, the conditions for water to become musta'mal (used), the role of intention, and the difference of opinion between Abu Hanifa and other imams regarding when water becomes musta'mal. Specifically, the commentary clarifies that water becomes used upon separation from the body during wudu, but al-Tahawi and some Balkh scholars hold that it becomes used only after it settles. The practical difference occurs if the water drips onto another body part: according to the first view, that part cannot be washed with it; according to the second, it can. The passage then discusses water from trees and fruits: it is not permissible to use for wudu even if it exudes without pressing, because it is not mutlaq water, except in the case of water that drips naturally (like from vine, but the text says the apparent view is that it is not allowed). Similarly, water whose nature has changed by cooking (e.g., chickpea water) or by being overcome by another substance loses its purifying quality. The second half of the passage addresses 'ghalabat al-ma' (water being overcome) by solids and liquids. The rule: if a solid (like saffron, fruit) mixes with water, even if it changes all three properties (color, taste, smell), it does not prevent the water from being purifying as long as it remains fluid and is not thickened to the point where it no longer flows. However, if a liquid with two attributes (like milk: color and taste) or three (like vinegar) mixes and overcomes one or two attributes, then wudu is not valid. For liquids without attributes (like used water or rose water that lost its smell), the criterion is weight: if the used water is more in weight, wudu is invalid; if the pure water is more, it is valid. If they are equal, the ruling is not explicitly in Zahir al-Riwayah; precaution is advised by following the overcome ruling. Finally, the fourth category of impure water is defined: water in which impurity falls, whether known with certainty or by dominant probability. The commentary notes that this applies to wells except for small droppings (like bird droppings) which are handled differently.

Key Terms
ماء مطلق (māʾ muṭlaq): Water that has not been mixed with anything that would make it restricted (muqayyad); it retains its absolute nature and is purifying.
ماء مستعمل (māʾ mustaʿmal): Water used for wudu or ghusl, either for removing hadath or for a qurbah (devotional act); it becomes pure in itself but not purifying for ritual impurity.
الغلبة (al-ghalabah): The state where a substance (solid or liquid) overcomes water in quantity or effect, either changing its properties or making it lose its fluidity, thus removing its purifying quality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in this passage, which of the following waters is considered 'mutaḥhir' (purifying) for wudu?
A) Water used once for wudu (musta'mal)
B) Water that has lost its fluidity by mixing with a solid
C) Water in which a cat (domestic) has drunk, in small quantity
D) Water mixed with milk such that the milk's taste and color appear

Key Takeaway
The five categories of water and the precise rules for ghalabah form the basis for determining the validity of wudu and other purifications in Hanafi fiqh.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
Defining Ghalaba and Categories of Water
Defines ghalaba and lists water types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ghalaba for solids and liquids and identify which water types (tahur, najis, mashkuk) apply in given scenarios.

Core Idea
Ghalaba removes water's purifying quality; its definition depends on whether the mixture is solid or liquid.

Water is the primary purifier in Islamic law, but its purifying quality can be lost when it mixes with other substances. The Hanafi school has detailed rules, known as ghalaba, to determine when this happens. In this lesson, we dissect these definitions and the resulting water categories.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two contexts of ghalaba
Ghalaba is defined separately for mixing with solids and with liquids. The key is to identify which category the added substance falls into.
Solids include saffron, fruit, leaves; liquids include milk, vinegar, rose water.
2
Apply ghalaba rule for solids
For solids, water is overpowered only if it loses its fluidity (rakikah) and flowability (sayalan). Change in color, taste, or smell does not harm as long as the water remains fluid.
Loss of fluidity means the water becomes thick or gelatinous.
3
Apply ghalaba rule for liquids
For liquids, the rule depends on the number of attributes (color, taste, smell) of the mixed liquid. If the liquid has two attributes, appearance of one attribute makes the water non-purifying. If it has three, appearance of two does so. If it has no attributes, overpowering is determined by weight.
Example: milk (color, taste) – one attribute appears → not purifying. Vinegar (color, taste, smell) – two attributes appear → not purifying. Used water (no attributes) – weight determines.
4
Identify the resulting water categories
After mixing, water can be tahur (purifying), najis (impure), or mashkuk (uncertain). Impure water is when impurity falls into it, with conditions based on quantity and flow. Uncertain water is from donkey or mule drink.
Pure water remains tahur if not overpowered and not contaminated by impurity.
5
Apply the rules to a scenario
Take a concrete case: water mixed with a solid, then with a liquid. Determine if ghalaba has occurred and what category the water falls into.
Practice with examples from the text.

The Book of Purification. 11. Issue of water overpowering. Issue of water overpowering. (Begin subject) And overpowering in mixing with solids is by removing water from its fluidity and flowability, and change of all its attributes by a solid such as saffron, fruit, and tree leaves does not harm. And overpowering in liquids is by the appearance of one attribute from a liquid that has only two attributes, like milk has color (begin subject) (predicate) (predicate fronted) (subject delayed) and taste but no smell, and by the appearance of two attributes from a liquid that has three like vinegar. And overpowering in a liquid (express declaration of what was previously known) (the subject mixing) that has no attribute like used water and rose water whose smell has ceased is by weight (predicate). For if two ratls of used water are mixed with one ratl of absolute water, wudu with it is not permissible, and in the opposite (interpretation of overpowering by weight for example) it is permissible. And the fourth: impure water, which is water in which impurity has fallen (the fourth division of waters). Overpowering of restricted water. And overpowering: beginning of detailing overpowering in the form of the rule, for overpowering differs according to the mixture without cooking its fluidity such that it does not seep from the garment's flow, meaning it does not flow over the limbs like the flow of water. And it does not harm, i.e., does not prevent the permissibility of wudu with it, if something pure mixes with it without cooking, with a solid that freezes water and every liquid freezing in the sense of solid and thickness opposite of melting. Fruit like [صاحبة؟] all fruits, not only dates, grapes, pomegranates. One attribute like color only or taste only. Only: and it has no third attribute. Like milk: if neither is found, wudu with it is permissible, and if one of them is found, it is not permissible, as if the mixture has one attribute and its attribute appears, like some watermelon and squash, for their water differs only in taste, and like rose water, for it differs only in smell. (Maraqi al-Falah and Hashiyat al-Tahtawi) Like vinegar has color, taste, and smell, so whichever two attributes appear prevent the validity of wudu, and one of them does not harm due to its scarcity. (Maraqi al-Falah: 26) Like used water: for by use it has not changed in taste, color, or smell, and we consider overpowering by weight here due to lack of distinction by attribute because it is absent. (Maraqi al-Falah with adaptation) Used water or rose water whose smell has ceased, and its opposite, which is if two ratls of absolute water and one ratl of used water or rose water whose smell has ceased are mixed, wudu with it is permissible. And if absolute and restricted water are equal, its ruling is not mentioned in the apparent narration, and the masters said its ruling is the ruling of the overpowered as a precaution. (Maraqi al-Falah) Impurity: [and its occurrence is known with certainty or by predominant assumption; the sheikh made it absolute, but it is restricted to other than small droppings; for the impurity of water by the fall of impurity therein is in other than small droppings if it falls in wells. (Hashiyat al-Tahtawi) The Book of Purification 12. And if it was stagnant, little - and little is less than ten by ten cubits - then it becomes impure even if its trace does not appear (connected) impurity therein, or flowing and its trace appears, and the trace is taste or color or smell. And the fifth: water (so it becomes impure) of impurity (meaning taste) uncertain in its purifying quality, which is water from which a donkey or mule has drunk (meaning not in its purity).
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on the definition of ghalaba (overpowering) when water mixes with other substances. It first distinguishes between mixing with solids and liquids. For solids, water is considered overpowered (and thus no longer purifying) only if it loses its fluidity and flowability; mere change in color, taste, or smell does not harm as long as it remains fluid. For liquids, the rule depends on the number of attributes (color, taste, smell) of the liquid it mixes with. If the liquid has only two attributes (like milk: color and taste, no smell), the appearance of even one attribute from that liquid in the water makes it overpowered. If the liquid has three attributes (like vinegar), the appearance of two renders it overpowered. For liquids with no attributes (like used water or rose water that lost smell), overpowering is determined by weight: if the quantity of the non-absolute water is greater, the mixture is not purifying. The text then lists the fourth type of water: impure water (najis), which is water into which impurity has fallen and it is either stagnant and less than 10x10 cubits, or flowing with a trace of the impurity. Finally, it mentions a fifth type: water of uncertain purifying quality (mashkuk), which is water drunk by a donkey or mule. The commentary emphasizes that ghalaba varies by context and provides examples from Maraqi al-Falah and Hashiyat al-Tahtawi.

Key Terms
الغلبة (al-ghalaba): The condition where water is overpowered by a mixture to the extent that it loses its purifying quality.
ماء نجس (ma' najis): Water in which impurity has fallen, and it is either stagnant and less than 10x10 cubits without trace showing, or flowing with trace showing.
ماء مشكوك (ma' mashkuk): Water of uncertain purifying quality, such as water from which a donkey or mule has drunk.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a liquid has two attributes (e.g., milk has color and taste but no smell), how many attributes must appear in the water for it to be considered overpowered?
A) One attribute
B) Both attributes
C) No attributes
D) Any change in one attribute is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
Ghalaba depends on context: solids require loss of fluidity, liquids require attribute appearance or weight ratio; understanding this protects the validity of wudu.

The Four Categories of Su'r (Leftover Water)
Classifies su'r into four categories based on the animal that drank.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four categories of su'r and identify the ruling for each category.

Core Idea
The ruling of su'r depends on the animal: pure for humans and halal animals, impure for dogs and pigs, doubtful for donkeys and mules, and makruh for jallalah animals.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi classification of leftover water (su'r). The Shariah differentiates between animals based on their inherent purity or impurity, and also considers the condition of the drinker's mouth. Understanding these categories is essential for knowing whether water can be used for purification.

Guided Path
1
Define su'r and its scope
Su'r is the leftover water from a person or animal after drinking, but only applies to little water (less than 10x10 cubits). For large bodies like rivers, the term is not used.
This definition is unique to the Hanafi school.
2
List the four categories
The passage divides su'r into: (1) pure and purifying (tahir mutahhir), (2) impure by consensus (najis), (3) doubtful (mashkuk), and (4) disliked (makruh) for jallalah animals.
The third category is implied for animals like donkeys and mules.
3
State the ruling for each category
For (1) humans and halal animals: pure and can be used for wudu. For (2) dogs and pigs: severely impure, cannot be used. For (3) donkeys and mules: doubtful – avoid if possible, combine wudu with tayammum if needed. For (4) jallalah: disliked but not impure.
Jallalah animals eat filth; their su'r is makruh.
4
Note the conditions and exceptions
For humans, the mouth must be free of impurity; if impure, see the second lesson. For halal animals, if they are jallalah, the ruling changes to disliked. For the doubtful category, the text narrates a difference of opinion on whether rulings can be uncertain.
Abu al-Tahir al-Dabbas argued that Shariah rulings cannot be uncertain, but the recommendation to exercise caution remains.
5
Apply the classification practically
When encountering a small water source, identify the animal that drank from it. Apply the appropriate ruling to determine if the water can be used for purification.
If uncertain, default to caution.

Chapter on the explanation of the rulings of leftover water. The second: impure by consensus, its use is not permissible, like camels, cows, and sheep (water of uncertain type) Abu al-Tahir al-Dabbas used to deny this statement and say: It is not permissible that any of the rulings of the Sharia be uncertain, but its meaning is that one should be cautious about it, so do not perform wudu with it in a state of choice, and if one does not find other water, one combines between it and tayammum. (Hashiyat al-Shalabi) or a mule and its mother was a donkey, not a mare, because consideration is given to the mother. And little water: which is not ten by ten and is not flowing. Su'r: with a hamza on its 'ayn. As for 'sur' without hamza: the building surrounding the city, plural aswar, and plural of su'r is as'ar. They said: it is only called su'r if it is little, so one does not say for something like a river drunk from that it is su'r. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi and Maraqi al-Falah summarized] Purifying: without dislike in its use. He made it absolute. Human: and it is restricted to when there is no impurity in his mouth. There is no difference between adult and child, Muslim and disbeliever, menstruating woman and junub. And if his mouth becomes impure, such as if he drank wine, or ate or drank something impure, or vomited mouthful, then he drinks water immediately, it becomes impure. But if after repeatedly moving saliva in his mouth and spitting it out or swallowing it before drinking, then his su'r is not impure according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but it is disliked, because of Muhammad's statement that impurity is not purified by saliva according to him. [Maraqi al-Falah: 29] Or what is eaten, etc. And there is no dislike in the su'r of what is eaten meat of if it is not a jallalah (that eats filth). As for jallalah, its su'r is from the third category, disliked. [Maraqi al-Falah: 29] And the second: from the divisions of little water is impure in speech, a type of summary. Know that the su'r of the dog and pig is severely impure by consensus. As for the su'r of others, it is severe impurity, and it is said: light. It is not permissible, i.e., purification with it is not valid in any case, and no one drinks it except one forced like carrion. [Maraqi al-Falah: 29]
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the rulings of su'r (leftover water) from animals. Su'r is defined as the leftover water from a person or animal after drinking, but only applies to small quantities (not rivers). The first category is pure and purifying: water drunk by a human (provided his mouth is not impure), a horse, or any animal whose meat is eaten (e.g., cows, sheep, goats, camels). There is no dislike unless the animal is a jallalah (one that eats filth). The second category is impure by consensus: the su'r of dogs and pigs is severely impure. The third category (implied) is water that is mashkuk (uncertain) - such as that drunk by a donkey or mule - about which one should exercise caution: avoid using it when other water is available, but if necessary, perform both wudu and tayammum as a precaution. The text also notes that if a person has impurity in his mouth (e.g., from drinking wine or vomiting), his su'r becomes impure according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but if he clears his mouth repeatedly, his su'r is not impure, though disliked according to Imam Muhammad.

Key Terms
السؤر (al-su'r): Leftover water after a person or animal has drunk from it, but only for small quantities (less than 10x10 cubits).
جلالة (jallalah): An animal that eats filth (impure substances) such that its meat and milk are affected.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Pure and purifying
B) Makruh but usable
C) Severely impure (najis ghaliz) by consensus
D) Uncertain (mashkuk)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies su'r into pure, impure, doubtful, and disliked, based on the animal's inherent purity and diet.

The Hanafi Dispute on Su'r After Clearing Impure Mouth
Presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the purity of su'r after clearing impure mouth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad regarding the su'r of a person who clears his mouth after impurity, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
After clearing the mouth of impurity, the su'r is pure but disliked according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, while Imam Muhammad holds it remains impure.

An important intra-Hanafi dispute arises when a person has impurity in his mouth (e.g., from drinking wine) and then clears his mouth by repeatedly moving his saliva and spitting it out. Is the water he then drinks considered pure? The three imams differ, and this lesson examines their reasoning and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The scenario is a person whose mouth becomes impure (e.g., drinking wine, eating najis, vomiting mouthful). He then clears his mouth by moving saliva around and spitting it out (or swallowing it) before drinking water.
This is not about immediate drinking after impurity, but after deliberate clearing.
2
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: the su'r is not impure, but it is disliked (makruh). Imam Muhammad: the su'r remains impure (najis).
The text from Maraqi al-Falah mentions this difference.
3
Analyze the basis for each
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf believe that the mouth is purified by repeated movement and expulsion of saliva, so the original impurity is removed. Imam Muhammad holds that impurity cannot be fully removed by saliva; traces remain.
The proof for Muhammad is that saliva itself is not a purifying agent.
4
Determine the mufta bihi and practical impact
The relied-upon view is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: the water is pure but makruh. In practice, one can use such water for wudu if needed, but it is better to avoid it.
This is the position of the majority of later Hanafi scholars.
